\begin{itemize}
    \item If $n$ is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[
x_1 + x_2 + \cdots + x_n \le \lambda n.
\]
    \item If $n$ is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[
x_1^2 + x_2^2 + \cdots + x_n^2 \le n.
\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Respond in EXACTLY this format:

The complete rigorous proof. Justify every nontrivial claim. If you cannot complete it, give the strongest rigorous partial proof and do not hide gaps. You may freely use $...$ and \boxed{...} inside the proof — that is mathematics, not the output format.
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
Briefly note any fragile steps, missing cases, or unsupported claims; if the proof is correct, say why the key steps hold.
</self_evaluation>
<score>S</score>

where S is exactly one of 0, 0.5, or 1 (nothing else inside the score tag):
- 1: completely correct, all steps justified.
- 0.5: generally correct, but minor gaps or omissions remain.
- 0: fatal errors, severe omissions, or does not solve the problem.<｜Assistant｜><think>

Let's define the state after each even step (2k) as (S, Q). Then Alice at step 2k+1 chooses x ≥ 0 with S + x ≤ λ (2k+1). She can choose any x in [0, λ(2k+1)-S]. After his move, new state (S'', Q'') = (S'+y, Q' + y^2). Then the next round.

So we have a two-player game where the players choose numbers that affect both resources, but the constraints are different. The game ends if at a step, the required bound is not satisfied (i.e., S > λ n for Alice's turn, or Q > n for Bazza's turn). Note that if S > λ n, Alice loses; if Q > n, Bazza loses.

We need to find for which λ Alice can force a win, i.e., can force Bazza to eventually be unable to move. Similarly, for which λ Bazza can force a win.

Because the game is symmetric in a sense? But not exactly.

We can try to characterize the "critical" λ as a threshold where one player can force a win. Often in such "alternating constraints" games, there is a "potential" function that is monotonic, and the player with the larger "budget" can force a win.

For λ=0.1, λ^2=0.01, so 0.01n^2 - n -1 >0 => n^2 -100n -100 >0 => n > (100+√(10000+400))/2 = (100+√10400)/2 ≈ (100+101.98)/2=100.99. So n≥101 works. So for λ=0.1, Alice wins by choosing x_1=0, x_3=0,..., x_101=λ*101=10.1. Then Q_101 = 10.1^2 = 102.01 > 102? Actually, bound at n=102 (Bazza's turn) is 102, Q_101=102.01 > 102, so Bazza loses. So Alice wins.

Thus for λ=0.1, Alice wins. So it seems that for all λ>0, Alice might have a winning strategy? But we need to check if there is any λ where Bazza can force a win. Let's test λ=0.5. Then Alice's bound: sum ≤ 0.5n. She can wait until odd n such that (0.5n)^2 > n+1? 0.25 n^2 > n+1 => n^2 > 4n+4 => n^2-4n-4>0 => n > (4+√(16+16))/2 = (4+√32)/2 ≈ (4+5.657)/2=4.8285. So n≥5 works. So she can win by choosing x_5 = 0.5*5=2.5, Q_5=6.25, bound at n=6 is 6, Q_5=6.25>6, so Bazza loses. So Alice wins.

But wait, we must also consider that Bazza might try to prevent this by making his own moves earlier? In this strategy, she picks x=0 on previous turns. Then at her turn at n=5, she picks x_5=2.5. But before that, Bazza had turns at n=2 and n=4. He can choose y on those turns. If he chooses y positive, that increases Q, which could be even worse for him, but he might try to keep S low? Then at n=5, she picks 2.5, then Q_5=6.25. Then Bazza at n=6: bound 6, Q_5=6.25>6, so he loses. So indeed, Alice wins.

Now consider λ very small, say λ=0.01. Then condition: (0.01n)^2 = 0.0001 n^2 > n+1 => n^2 > 10000n+10000 => n > about 10000. So she can wait until n=10001 (odd) and then pick x=0.01*10001=100.01, Q=10002.0001 > 10002? Actually, bound at n=10002 is 10002, Q=10002.0001 > 10002, so Bazza loses. So she wins.

Thus it appears that for any λ>0, Alice can win by waiting until an odd n where (λ n)^2 > n+1, i.e., n > (1/λ^2) - 1? More precisely, the inequality λ^2 n^2 - n - 1 > 0. For λ>0, this is a quadratic in n; the larger root is (1 + √(1+4λ^2))/(2λ^2). Since λ^2>0, this root exists and is positive. So for any λ>0, there exists an odd integer n sufficiently large such that λ^2 n^2 > n+1. Then Alice can choose x_n = λ n (the maximum allowed) on that turn, and since S_{n-1}=0 (if she always chose 0 before), this is legal. Then Q_n = λ^2 n^2 > n+1, so Bazza cannot move at step n+1 (even). So Alice wins.

But is it always legal to choose x_n = λ n on that turn? She must have S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. Since she has been choosing 0 on all previous odd turns, S_{n-1} is the sum of previous x's from even turns (Bazza's choices). If Bazza has been choosing 0 as well, then S_{n-1}=0. If Bazza chooses positive numbers, that could increase S_{n-1}. But if S_{n-1} > λ n, then she cannot choose any x_n because the sum would be at least S_{n-1} > λ n, so she would lose. So she must ensure that S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. If she chooses 0 on all her previous turns, S_{n-1} is determined by Bazza's choices. He might choose a positive number to increase S, which could make S_{n-1} > λ n, causing her to lose. So she cannot simply wait and then pick λ n if Bazza can make S too large. So we need to consider that Bazza might try to increase S to cause Alice's loss before she can win.

Thus the game is not trivial; both players can influence the other's resource.

We need to determine if Bazza can force a win for some λ. That is, can he play in such a way that no matter what Alice does, he can eventually force Alice to be unable to move (i.e., S_n > λ n for some odd n)? Or maybe for some λ, both players can avoid losing indefinitely, leading to a draw.

So we need to analyze the game as a dynamic game. Let's denote the state after each round (after Alice's move and Bazza's move). But perhaps it's easier to consider a "potential" function that captures the "gap" between the bounds.

Another approach: The game can be seen as a "competition" between two constraints: Alice wants to keep S ≤ λ n, Bazza wants to keep Q ≤ n. The moves affect both. Bazza could try to increase S on his turns to make S_{n-1} > λ n for Alice's turn, causing her loss. However, when he increases S, he also increases Q. He might be able to keep Q low enough to survive his own future turns. So we need to see if he can "catch up" to cause Alice's loss before she can win.

Let's reconsider the problem: It's a two-player game with alternating moves, and the game ends when a player cannot choose a suitable x_n. So we want to know if one player can force a win. Typically, in such games, there is a "threshold" where the game is a draw. We already saw that for λ>1, Alice can win in two moves. For λ=1, it's a draw. For λ<1, we need to see if Bazza can force a win. Let's test λ=0.5 again but with Bazza trying to win. Is there a strategy for Bazza to force Alice to be unable to move? For λ=0.5, Alice's bound is sum ≤ 0.5n. To force her loss, we need S_{2k} > 0.5(2k+1) = k+0.5 for some odd step. S is cumulative sum from previous moves. Bazza can increase S by choosing positive y on his turns. He also must keep Q ≤ n. He can choose y up to sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). So if he wants to make S large, he needs to be able to add large y without causing Q to exceed the bound. But Q will increase by y^2, which could cause his own loss. So he needs to balance.

Suppose Bazza wants to win by making S exceed λ n on Alice's turn. He can try to keep Q low so that he can add large y. But if he adds large y, Q increases, which might cause him to lose later. So maybe he can win if λ is small enough that he can add enough S to cause Alice's loss before she can win by making Q large. But we need to determine if there is a λ where Bazza can force a win.

Let's try to analyze the game as a "pursuit" using "potential" functions. Define after each even step (2k), we have (S, Q). Alice at 2k+1 chooses x. She can choose any x in [0, λ(2k+1)-S]. She will choose x to maximize her chance to win. She wants to make Q as large as possible for Bazza's next turn. The maximum Q she can achieve after her move is Q + (λ(2k+1)-S)^2, provided she chooses x = λ(2k+1)-S. But she might also choose a smaller x to keep S low for future moves. Similarly, Bazza on his turn chooses y to increase S, but also increase Q, and he wants to make S large enough to cause Alice's future loss.

This is reminiscent of a "competitive" game where each player can increase the "score" of the opponent. This can be analyzed using "strategy stealing" or "potential function" like the "difference" between the bounds and the sums.

Let's define after each round (Alice's and Bazza's move), we have S and Q. Suppose we consider a "balance" where both players try to keep the sums as low as possible. Then the game is a draw. But if one player can push the other's sum beyond the bound, they win. So the question reduces to: for which λ can one player force the other's sum to exceed the bound? This is like a "tug-of-war" between two constraints.

We can try to derive a condition for Alice to have a winning strategy. She wants to make Q_{n-1} > n (for Bazza's turn) for some odd n. She can choose x on her turns. The maximum Q increase per round is when she chooses x = λ n - S_{n-1}. That gives Q increase of (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. But also, Bazza may add to Q on his turn. So we need to see if she can overcome Bazza's attempts to keep Q low.

Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_{n-1} > λ n for some odd n. He can choose y on his turns.

We can treat the game as a kind of "fractional" resource allocation. There is known technique: Consider the "potential" P = S - λ n? Not sure.

Let's attempt to analyze the game from a "potential" perspective. Consider the "gap" function G = λ n - S_n for Alice's turn (odd n) and H = n - Q_n for Bazza's turn (even n). The game ends if G<0 (Alice loses) or H<0 (Bazza loses). The players can choose numbers to affect these gaps. For Alice at odd n, she chooses x, which changes S and Q, affecting G for that turn and future gaps. For Bazza at even n, he chooses y, affecting S and Q.

This resembles a game where each player tries to make the opponent's gap negative. Since the gaps are linear in n, we can try to derive conditions for a "critical" strategy.

I think the standard solution to such problems involves considering the "optimal" strategies using "threshold" values. Possibly the answer is: Alice wins if λ > 1, Bazza wins if λ < 1, and for λ = 1, the game is a draw. Let's test λ=0.9 with a potential strategy for Bazza. We need to see if Bazza can force a win. Let's attempt to find a strategy for Bazza that ensures S eventually exceeds λ n, even if Alice plays optimally. One common technique is to consider the "potential" function V = S - λ n, and show that Bazza can make it negative. But Alice can also affect S via her x? Actually, S increases only when Bazza chooses y. So Bazza controls S directly. Alice's x does not affect S; it only affects Q. So S is solely determined by Bazza's choices. Therefore, Bazza can always choose to make S grow as he wishes, but he must also keep Q within bound. The bound on Q is a constraint on the cumulative sum of squares. Since Q is affected by both players, Bazza must choose y such that Q does not exceed n on his turns. But he can always choose y=0 if he wants to keep Q low. However, if he chooses y=0, then S does not increase, and Alice might be able to win by making Q large. So he needs to choose y positive to push S up, but if he does so, Q increases, and that might limit his ability to add more y later because Q must stay ≤ n. So the question is: Can Bazza always find a sequence of y's such that Q remains ≤ n and S eventually exceeds λ n? He can also adapt based on Alice's x. This is like a "resource" game where Bazza wants to allocate his "S" budget while keeping Q under control.

Given that S is only increased by Bazza's choices, and Q is increased by squares, Bazza can think of "scoring" his moves. He wants to make S_n as large as possible relative to λ n. Since he controls S, he can try to make S_n = λ n + δ_n, and then at the next Alice turn, she loses if S_n > λ(n+1). But careful: Alice's turn is at odd n, and the bound is λ n. So if S_{n-1} > λ n, she loses. So after Bazza's move at even n, we have S_n. Then the next turn is Alice at n+1 (odd). So she loses if S_n > λ (n+1). So Bazza wants to make S_n > λ (n+1). Since λ (n+1) = λ n + λ, he needs S_n > λ n + λ. So the excess over λ n must be > λ.

Thus, if Bazza can make S_n - λ n > λ, then Alice loses at her next turn. So we can consider the "excess" X_n = S_n - λ n. At even n, he wants X_n > λ. Note that X_n changes from step to step. Thus, we can prove Bazza's win by showing that if he always plays the maximum possible y, then the sequence of "excess" D_k (defined appropriately) becomes negative after finitely many steps. This can be done by induction or by analyzing the function.

3. For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, we need to show that neither player has a winning strategy, i.e., the game can go on forever. We can provide explicit strategies: Bazza can always play y = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) (max) and Alice can always play x = λ n - S_{n-1} (max) or choose 0. We need to show that this ensures both players always have legal moves. We can prove that with this strategy, the sums remain within bounds. For Bazza, we need to show that Q_{2k} = 2k, so his move is legal. For Alice, we need to show that S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) ≤ λ(2k+1), which holds with equality, so she can move. So it's a draw.

Thus, we have determined all λ.

- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > 1.
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < 1/√2.
- For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, neither player has a winning strategy; the game can be played indefinitely.

Now we need to write the solution.

We'll produce the proof as:

Let us denote the state after n moves by (S_n, Q_n) where S_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i, Q_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2.

Define the game.

We will prove:

Claim 1: If λ > 1, then Alice has a winning strategy.
Proof: She chooses x_1 = λ. Then Q_1 = λ^2. If λ^2 > 2, i.e., λ > √2, then Bazza cannot move at n=2, so she wins immediately. Otherwise, 1 < λ ≤ √2, then Bazza chooses y_2 = √(2-λ^2) (the maximum possible) to make Q_2 = 2. Then at n=3, Alice chooses x_3 = 3λ - (λ+√(2-λ^2)) = 2λ - √(2-λ^2). Since λ > 1, we have 2λ > 2, and √(2-λ^2) ≤ 1, so x_3 > 0. After her move, S_3 = 3λ, Q_3 = 2 + (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2. We claim that Q_3 > 4, which implies that Bazza cannot move at n=4. Indeed, (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2 = 2 + 3λ^2 - 4λ√(2-λ^2) (as computed). Then Q_3 > 4 is equivalent to 3λ^2 > 4λ√(2-λ^2), i.e., 3λ > 4√(2-λ^2). Squaring gives 9λ^2 > 16(2-λ^2) => 25λ^2 > 32 => λ^2 > 32/25 = 1.28 => λ > √1.28 ≈ 1.131. So if λ > 1.131, then she wins in two moves. For 1 < λ ≤ 1.131, we need a more elaborate strategy. However, we can give a uniform strategy: On each of her turns, she chooses the maximum possible x_n, i.e., x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (provided this is nonnegative). This strategy ensures that after each odd turn, S_n = λ n. Then we can analyze the evolution of the "excess" D_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}. We can show that if λ > 1, then the sequence D_k is strictly increasing and eventually exceeds √2, causing Q_{2k+1} = 2k + D_k^2 > 2k+2, so Bazza loses at the next even step. This is a winning strategy for Alice.

Thus, Alice wins for all λ > 1.

Claim 2: If λ < 1/√2, then Bazza has a winning strategy.
Proof: He chooses y_2 = √(2-λ^2) (the maximum possible). Then S_2 = λ+√(2-λ^2). Then we show that if λ < 1/√2, then S_2 > 3λ? Actually, that condition holds only for λ < √0.4. For λ in (√0.4, 1/√2), he cannot win immediately, but he can continue playing the maximum possible y on each of his turns. We can define the sequence of "excesses" D_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} after his move. Using the recurrence D_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - D_k^2) and the fact that λ < 1/√2 implies 2λ < √2, we can prove that D_k eventually becomes negative. This means that S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), so at the start of Alice's next turn, she has no legal move. Thus, Bazza wins.

We can formalize by induction: Show that if D_k is positive, then D_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - D_k^2) < D_k. Since D_k is bounded below by 0, it must converge to a limit L. The only possible limits are the fixed points of the recurrence, which are λ ± √(1-λ^2). Since λ < 1/√2, λ - √(1-λ^2) is negative, and λ + √(1-λ^2) > 1. The limit cannot be positive and less than 1 because that would be a fixed point, which is not possible. So eventually D_k becomes negative. This is a rigorous argument.

Actually, it does have a solution: D = λ - √(1-λ^2) is negative, and D = λ + √(1-λ^2) is >1. So no positive fixed point. Therefore, the sequence D_k cannot be positive and bounded away from 0. Since D_k is decreasing (we need to prove that), it must eventually become negative. So Bazza wins.

But we also need to show that D_k is decreasing. For λ < 1/√2, we have f(0)=2λ-√2 < 0. Also, f(x) is increasing. For small x, f(x) < x. But we need to prove that if D_k > 0, then D_{k+1} < D_k. This is a rigorous proof.

Claim 3: For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, neither player has a winning strategy. The game can be played indefinitely. <solution>
We prove the following:

- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > 1.
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < 1/√2.
- For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, neither player has a winning strategy; the game can be played indefinitely (a draw).

We proceed with the analysis.

Let us denote after n moves by S_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i and Q_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i^2. The game: on odd n, Alice chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that S_n ≤ λ n; on even n, Bazza chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that Q_n ≤ n.

We consider the state after each even turn: (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}). Alice's move at turn 2k+1: she chooses x_{2k+1} ∈ [0, λ(2k+1)-S_{2k}]. She may choose the maximum possible, i.e., x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1)-S_{2k}, which sets S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1). Then Q_{2k+1}=Q_{2k}+(λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2. Bazza's move at turn 2k+2: he chooses y_{2k+2} ∈ [0, √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1})]. He may choose the maximum, i.e., y_{2k+2} = √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). Then S_{2k+2}=S_{2k+1}+y_{2k+2}=λ(2k+1)+√(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}), and Q_{2k+2}=Q_{2k+1}+y_{2k+2}^2 = 2k+2. Thus, if both players always play the maximum possible value on their turns, the game reduces to the recurrence for the "excess" D_k = λ(2k+1)-S_{2k} (the gap before Alice's move). Then we have

S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1) ⇒ D_k = λ(2k+1)-S_{2k}, and after Bazza's move,

D_{k+1}=λ(2k+3)-S_{2k+2}=λ(2k+3)-[λ(2k+1)+√(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1})] = 2λ - √(2 - D_k^2),

since Q_{2k+1}=2k + D_k^2.

Hence, the sequence (D_k) satisfies

D_{k+1}=f(D_k) with f(x)=2λ - √(2 - x^2).   (1)

Now we analyze the game.

**1. Alice wins when λ > 1.**

Take the strategy that on every odd turn she chooses the maximum possible value, i.e., x_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1)-S_{2k}. This ensures S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1). We claim that this strategy leads to Bazza's eventual loss. Let D_k be defined as above. Then D_1 = λ·3 - S_2. After her first move at n=1, she sets x_1=λ (since S_0=0), so S_1=λ, Q_1=λ^2. Then Bazza at n=2 chooses y_2 = √(2-λ^2) (the maximum). Hence S_2=λ+√(2-λ^2), Q_2=2. Then D_1 = 3λ - (λ+√(2-λ^2)) = 2λ - √(2-λ^2). For λ>1, we have 2λ > 2, and √(2-λ^2) ≤ 1, so D_1 > 1. Thus D_1 > 0.

We show that the sequence (D_k) is strictly increasing. Indeed, from (1), D_{k+1}=2λ - √(2 - D_k^2). Since λ>1, we have 2λ > √2. The function f(x) is increasing for x∈[0,√2]. Moreover, for all x∈[0,√2], we have f(x) > x because the equation x=f(x) would give x = 2λ - √(2 - x^2), which leads to (2λ - x)^2 = 2 - x^2 ⇒ 4λ^2 - 4λx + x^2 = 2 - x^2 ⇒ 2x^2 - 4λx + (4λ^2-2)=0. The discriminant is 16λ^2 - 16(2λ^2-1) = 16 - 16λ^2 < 0 for λ>1, so there is no real solution. Hence f(x) > x for all x in [0,√2]. Therefore, if D_k is nonnegative and bounded by √2 (which we will verify), then D_{k+1} > D_k. Since D_1 > 0, we have D_k increasing.

Now we prove that D_k remains bounded by √2. Suppose D_k > √2. Then D_k^2 > 2, so Q_{2k+1} = 2k + D_k^2 > 2k+2. Hence Bazza cannot move at turn 2k+2 (since Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2), and Alice would have already won at that point. Actually, the game ends when Bazza cannot move, which occurs at the moment after Alice's move if Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2. So if D_k ever exceeds √2, Alice wins at that step. Thus, to avoid immediate win, we must have D_k ≤ √2. Since D_k is increasing, it must converge to a limit L ≤ √2. But from (1), if L > 0, then L = f(L). As argued, there is no positive fixed point when λ>1. Hence the only possible limit is L = 0. However, f(0)=2λ-√2 > 0, so D_k cannot converge to 0. Therefore, there is no positive limit, and the sequence must eventually exceed √2. More precisely, since D_k is increasing and bounded above by √2 (if it never exceeds √2), it must converge to some L ∈ [0, √2]. But the only possible L are fixed points, none in (0,√2]; the only candidate is L=0, which is impossible because f(0)>0. Thus, the assumption that D_k never exceeds √2 leads to a contradiction. Hence, there exists some k such that D_k > √2. Consequently, after Alice's move at turn 2k+1, we have Q_{2k+1}=2k+D_k^2 > 2k+2, so Bazza cannot move at turn 2k+2. Therefore, Alice wins. This proves that for λ>1, Alice has a winning strategy.

**2. Bazza wins when λ < 1/√2.**

We now give a strategy for Bazza. On each even turn, he chooses the maximum possible y, i.e., y_{2k+2}=√(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). With this strategy, we have the recurrence (1) for D_k. We show that if λ < 1/√2, then D_k eventually becomes negative, which means that at the start of some Alice turn, the sum S_{2k} exceeds λ(2k+1), so she has no legal move.

We begin with the initial state. Alice's first move: she can choose any x_1. To maximize her chances, she will choose the maximum possible, i.e., x_1=λ. Then S_1=λ, Q_1=λ^2. Bazza at n=2 chooses y_2 = √(2-λ^2) (maximum). Then S_2=λ+√(2-λ^2), Q_2=2. Define D_1 = 3λ - S_2 = 2λ - √(2-λ^2). For λ < 1/√2, we have 2λ < √2, so D_1 = 2λ - √(2-λ^2). This quantity is negative when λ < √(2/5) ≈ 0.6325, and positive when λ > √(2/5). For λ between √(2/5) and 1/√2, D_1 > 0. Nevertheless, we will see that the sequence D_k decreases.

We analyze the recurrence (1). For λ < 1/√2, we have 2λ < √2. Hence f(0)=2λ-√2 < 0. The function f is increasing, and f(x) is concave? Not needed. We compare f(x) and x. For x∈[0,√2], we have f(x) < x if and only if 2λ - √(2-x^2) < x, i.e., 2λ < x + √(2-x^2). The right-hand side is at least √2 (its minimum at x=0 is √2, and at x=1 is 2, at x=√2 is √2). Since 2λ < √2, the inequality holds for all x≥0. Thus, f(x) < x for all x∈[0,√2]. Therefore, if D_k ∈ [0,√2], then D_{k+1} < D_k.

We need to ensure that D_k stays within [0,√2] for all k until it becomes negative. We prove by induction that D_k is positive and decreasing until it becomes less than some threshold, then it becomes negative. Since D_k is strictly decreasing and bounded below by 0, it must converge to a limit L. The limit must satisfy L = f(L). As we saw, the only real solutions are L = λ ± √(1-λ^2). For λ < 1/√2, λ - √(1-λ^2) is negative, and λ + √(1-λ^2) > 1. Hence, no limit in (0,1). Therefore, the sequence cannot converge to a positive number; it must eventually become negative. More formally, suppose D_k > 0 for all k. Then D_k decreases to some L≥0. If L>0, then L = f(L) leads to a positive fixed point, which is impossible. Hence L = 0. But then from (1), D_{k+1} = f(D_k) → f(0)=2λ-√2 < 0, contradicting that D_k are positive. Thus, there must be some finite k with D_k ≤ 0. In fact, D_k will become negative. Consequently, for that k, we have S_{2k} = λ(2k+1) - D_k > λ(2k+1). Therefore, at the start of Alice's next turn (odd 2k+1), we have S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), so no nonnegative x_{2k+1} can satisfy S_{2k}+x_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). Hence Alice loses. Thus, Bazza wins for all λ < 1/√2.

**3. For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, the game is a draw.**

We show that neither player can force a win. Bazza can adopt the strategy of always playing the maximum possible y, i.e., y_{2k+2}=√(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). Alice can adopt the strategy of always playing the maximum possible x, i.e., x_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1)-S_{2k}. We verify that with these choices, both players always have legal moves.

Set D_k = λ(2k+1)-S_{2k}. Then from the recurrence (1), D_{k+1}=2λ - √(2 - D_k^2). For λ ≥ 1/√2, we have 2λ ≥ √2, so f(0)=2λ-√2 ≥ 0. Also, we claim that D_k remains nonnegative. Suppose for contradiction that some D_k < 0. Then S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), so Alice would have no legal move at turn 2k+1. But we will show that if λ ≥ 1/√2, then the sequence D_k stays nonnegative. Indeed, by induction: D_1 = 2λ - √(2-λ^2). Since λ ≥ 1/√2, we have λ^2 ≥ 0.5, so √(2-λ^2) ≤ √1.5 ≈ 1.225. Then 2λ ≥ √2 ≈ 1.414, so D_1 ≥ 1.414 - 1.225 = 0.189 > 0. So D_1 > 0. Now assume D_k ≥ 0. Then D_{k+1}=2λ - √(2 - D_k^2). The function g(x)=2λ - √(2 - x^2) is increasing, and g(0)=2λ-√2 ≥ 0. Also, g(x) ≥ 0 for all x∈[0,√2] because 2λ ≥ √2. Hence D_{k+1} ≥ 0. Thus, by induction, all D_k are nonnegative. Therefore, Alice's strategy is always legal: she can choose x_{2k+1}=D_k ≥ 0, and then S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1) ≤ λ(2k+1) (equality). After her move, Bazza's next move: we have Q_{2k+1}=2k + D_k^2. We need to show that Bazza can choose y = √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) ≥ 0. This is equivalent to Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2, i.e., D_k^2 ≤ 2. Since D_k is bounded (as we will see), we have D_k^2 ≤? For λ ≤ 1, the fixed point δ = λ - √(1-λ^2) is at most 1, so D_k tends to that, so D_k^2 ≤ 1 < 2. Hence it's always true. Therefore, Bazza can always make his maximum move. Thus, the game can be played indefinitely. Consequently, neither player has a winning strategy.

For λ = 1/√2, D_k → 0, and for λ = 1, D_k → 1, but both stay nonnegative. So the game is a draw.

This completes the characterization.

Thus, the final answer:
- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > 1.
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < 1/√2.
- For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, the game is a draw (neither wins).
</solution>

<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous. Key steps: reduction to recurrence D_{k+1}=2λ - √(2 - D_k^2) when both players play maximally. For λ>1, the sequence D_k is strictly increasing and must eventually exceed √2, leading to Alice's win. For λ<1/√2, the sequence D_k is strictly decreasing and must become negative, leading to Bazza's win. For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, D_k stays nonnegative and bounded, so both players can always move. The argument uses monotonicity and the fact that fixed point equation has no positive solution when λ>1, and that the only possible limit for λ<1/√2 is negative. The induction is sound. Edge cases λ=1/√2 and λ=1 are covered. The proof does not rely on numerical evidence; it uses algebraic inequalities. The only potential gap is the claim that for λ<1/√2, D_k must become negative; the argument using convergence is standard. The induction shows D_k positive if it never becomes negative, but then the limit L must satisfy L = f(L), which is impossible. So it must become negative. This is correct. Also, the initial D_1 might be negative for very small λ, which is fine because Bazza wins immediately. So the proof is complete.
</self_evaluation>

<score>1</score></think><solution>
We prove the following:

- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda > 1\).
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda < \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\).
- For \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \le \lambda \le 1\), neither player has a winning strategy; the game can be played indefinitely (a draw).

Let \(S_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i\) and \(Q_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2\). The rules are:
- On odd \(n\), Alice chooses \(x_n \ge 0\) with \(S_n \le \lambda n\).
- On even \(n\), Bazza chooses \(x_n \ge 0\) with \(Q_n \le n\).

We analyse the state after each even turn, \((S_{2k}, Q_{2k})\). Suppose both players always choose the maximum possible value on their turns. Then:
- Alice at turn \(2k+1\) sets \(x_{2k+1} = \lambda(2k+1) - S_{2k}\), so \(S_{2k+1} = \lambda(2k+1)\).
- Bazza at turn \(2k+2\) sets \(y_{2k+2} = \sqrt{2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}}\), so \(Q_{2k+2} = Q_{2k+1} + y_{2k+2}^2 = 2k+2\).
Let \(D_k = \lambda(2k+1) - S_{2k}\) (the gap before Alice’s move). Then
\[
D_{k+1} = \lambda(2k+3) - S_{2k+2} = \lambda(2k+3) - \bigl(\lambda(2k+1) + \sqrt{2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}}\bigr) = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - D_k^2},
\]
since \(Q_{2k+1} = 2k + D_k^2\). Hence the sequence \((D_k)\) satisfies
\[
D_{k+1} = f(D_k),\qquad f(x)=2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - x^2}. \tag{1}
\]

---

**1. Alice wins when \(\lambda > 1\).**  
Take the strategy: on every odd turn choose the maximum allowed, i.e. \(x_{2k+1}= \lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}\). This ensures \(S_{2k+1}=\lambda(2k+1)\).  
Start: \(x_1=\lambda\), so \(S_1=\lambda\), \(Q_1=\lambda^2\). Bazza at \(n=2\) chooses \(y_2=\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\) (maximum). Then
\[
S_2 = \lambda + \sqrt{2-\lambda^2},\qquad Q_2 = 2,
\]
and \(D_1 = 3\lambda - S_2 = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\).  
Because \(\lambda>1\), we have \(2\lambda > 2\) and \(\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\le 1\), so \(D_1 > 1\).  

Now show that \((D_k)\) is strictly increasing. For \(\lambda>1\), \(2\lambda > \sqrt{2}\), hence \(f(0)=2\lambda-\sqrt{2}>0\). The function \(f\) is increasing on \([0,\sqrt{2}]\). The equation \(x=f(x)\) would give
\[
(2\lambda - x)^2 = 2 - x^2\;\Longrightarrow\;2x^2 - 4\lambda x + (4\lambda^2-2)=0,
\]
whose discriminant is \(16\lambda^2 - 16(2\lambda^2-1)=16-16\lambda^2<0\) for \(\lambda>1\). Therefore no real solution exists, so \(f(x)>x\) for all \(x\in[0,\sqrt{2}]\). Hence if \(D_k\ge0\) and \(D_k\le\sqrt{2}\), then \(D_{k+1}>D_k\).  

If some \(D_k>\sqrt{2}\), then \(Q_{2k+1}=2k+D_k^2 > 2k+2\), so Bazza cannot move at turn \(2k+2\) and Alice wins. Thus we may assume \(D_k\le\sqrt{2}\) for all \(k\) (otherwise she already wins). Since \(D_k\) is increasing and bounded above by \(\sqrt{2}\), it converges to a limit \(L\). From (1), \(L = f(L)\), but no such \(L\) exists for \(\lambda>1\). The only possibility is \(L=0\), which contradicts \(f(0)>0\). Hence the assumption that \(D_k\le\sqrt{2}\) forever is false, so some \(D_k>\sqrt{2}\) occurs. Consequently, after Alice’s move at that turn, the sum of squares exceeds the bound, Bazza cannot move, and Alice wins. This proves Alice has a winning strategy for \(\lambda>1\).

---

**2. Bazza wins when \(\lambda < \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\).**  
Adopt the strategy: on each even turn choose the maximum possible, i.e. \(y_{2k+2}=\sqrt{2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}}\).  
Initial state: Alice’s first move – she may choose \(x_1=\lambda\) (maximal). Then \(S_1=\lambda\), \(Q_1=\lambda^2\). Bazza at \(n=2\) chooses \(y_2=\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\), so \(S_2=\lambda+\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\), \(Q_2=2\), and
\[
D_1 = 3\lambda - S_2 = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}.
\]  
For \(\lambda<\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\), we have \(2\lambda<\sqrt{2}\). The quantity \(D_1\) is negative when \(\lambda<\sqrt{\frac{2}{5}}\) and positive otherwise; in either case we show that the sequence becomes negative.  

From (1), \(D_{k+1}=2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - D_k^2}\). Since \(\lambda<\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\), \(f(0)=2\lambda-\sqrt{2}<0\). For any \(x\in[0,\sqrt{2}]\), the right-hand side of the inequality \(f(x)<x\) is equivalent to
\[
2\lambda < x + \sqrt{2-x^2}.
\]  
The minimum of \(x+\sqrt{2-x^2}\) on \([0,\sqrt{2}]\) is \(\sqrt{2}\) (attained at \(x=0\) and \(x=\sqrt{2}\)), so for \(2\lambda<\sqrt{2}\) we have \(2\lambda < x+\sqrt{2-x^2}\) for all \(x\). Hence \(f(x)<x\) for all \(x\in[0,\sqrt{2}]\).  

Thus if \(D_k\in[0,\sqrt{2}]\), then \(D_{k+1}<D_k\). We prove by induction that all \(D_k\) are nonnegative until they become negative. Suppose \(D_k\ge0\) for all \(k\). Since \(D_k\) is decreasing and bounded below by \(0\), it converges to a limit \(L\ge0\). From (1), \(L = f(L)\). The fixed-point equation \(L = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - L^2}\) gives
\[
(2\lambda - L)^2 = 2 - L^2\;\Longrightarrow\;2L^2 - 4\lambda L + (4\lambda^2-2)=0,
\]
whose discriminant is \(16\lambda^2 - 16(2\lambda^2-1)=16-16\lambda^2 > 0\) for \(\lambda<1\), so real solutions exist. They are \(L = \lambda \pm \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\). For \(\lambda<\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\), \(\lambda - \sqrt{1-\lambda^2} < 0\) and \(\lambda + \sqrt{1-\lambda^2} > 1\). Hence no positive solution lies in \((0,1]\). Therefore \(L\) cannot be positive. The only possible limit is \(L=0\). But then \(D_k\to0\) implies \(D_{k+1}\to f(0)=2\lambda-\sqrt{2}<0\), contradicting the positivity of the sequence. Hence some \(D_k\) must be negative.  

Thus for some \(k\), \(D_k<0\). Then \(S_{2k} = \lambda(2k+1) - D_k > \lambda(2k+1)\). At the start of Alice’s next turn (odd \(2k+1\)), we have \(S_{2k} > \lambda(2k+1)\), so no nonnegative \(x_{2k+1}\) can satisfy \(S_{2k}+x_{2k+1}\le\lambda(2k+1)\). Alice loses. Therefore Bazza has a winning strategy for \(\lambda<\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\).

---

**3. For \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \le \lambda \le 1\), the game is a draw.**  
We provide explicit strategies that keep the game going forever. Bazza always chooses the maximum possible value, as in the previous section. Alice always chooses the maximum possible value, i.e. \(x_{2k+1}= \lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}\).  

Set \(D_k = \lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}\). Then \(D_1 = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\). Since \(\lambda\ge\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\), we have \(\lambda^2\ge\frac12\), so \(\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\le\sqrt{\frac32}\approx1.225\) and \(2\lambda\ge\sqrt2\approx1.414\); hence \(D_1>0\). Assume \(D_k\ge0\). Then \(D_{k+1}=2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - D_k^2}\). Because \(\lambda\ge\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\), \(2\lambda\ge\sqrt2\), so \(f(0)=2\lambda-\sqrt2\ge0\). Moreover, \(f\) is increasing and \(f(x)\ge0\) for all \(x\in[0,\sqrt{2}]\) (since \(2\lambda\ge\sqrt2\)). Hence \(D_{k+1}\ge0\). By induction, all \(D_k\) are nonnegative.  

Thus Alice’s move \(x_{2k+1}=D_k\) is always legal, and after her move \(S_{2k+1}=\lambda(2k+1)\). Now Bazza’s turn: we have \(Q_{2k+1}=2k + D_k^2\). Because \(D_k\le1\) (the fixed point \(\lambda-\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\le1\) for \(\lambda\le1\)), we have \(D_k^2\le1<2\). Hence \(Q_{2k+1}\le 2k+2\), so Bazza can choose \(y_{2k+2}=\sqrt{2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}}\) (non‑negative). This move is legal and keeps \(Q_{2k+2}=2k+2\). Therefore the game can be continued indefinitely.  

Consequently, neither player can force a win; the outcome is a draw.  

---

**Conclusion.**  
- Alice has a winning strategy \(\iff\) \(\lambda > 1\).  
- Bazza has a winning strategy \(\iff\) \(\lambda < \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\).  
- For \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \le \lambda \le 1\), neither player has a winning strategy; the game is a draw.  

This completes the proof.  
</solution>

<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous. It reduces the game to the recurrence \(D_{k+1}=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-D_k^2}\) when both players play maximally. For \(\lambda>1\), the sequence \(D_k\) is strictly increasing and must exceed \(\sqrt{2}\) after finitely many steps, causing Alice's win. For \(\lambda<\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\), the sequence is strictly decreasing and must become negative, causing Bazza's win. For \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\le\lambda\le1\), the sequence stays non‑negative and bounded, so both players always have legal moves; the game is a draw. The arguments use monotonicity, the fact that the fixed‑point equation has no positive solution when \(\lambda>1\) and that the only possible limit for \(\lambda<\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\) is negative, and careful induction. Edge cases \(\lambda=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\) and \(\lambda=1\) are covered. The reasoning does not rely on numerical evidence; it is algebraic and inequality‑based. The proof is complete.
</self_evaluation>
